        "solution": "Solution:\nAnswer approach — Align digits by place value (ones, tens, hundreds), add each column starting from the ones place, and carry to the next column when needed. Understanding what each digit represents (its place value) ensures you add like values together.\n\nSteps and reasoning:\n1. Identify place values: in [math]453[/math], the digits represent [math]4×100=400[/math], [math]5×10=50[/math], [math]3×1=3[/math]; in [math]325[/math], they are [math]3×100=300[/math], [math]2×10=20[/math], [math]5×1=5[/math].  \n2. Align and add column by column (ones → tens → hundreds):\n   - Ones: [math]3+5=8[/math].  \n   - Tens: [math]5+2=7[/math].  \n   - Hundreds: [math]4+3=7[/math].  \n3. Combine the results to get the total: [math]453+325=778[/math].\n\nRelevant concept:\n- Place value means a digit's contribution depends on its position (units, tens, hundreds). You must add digits in the same place-value column so you are combining equal values (e.g., tens with tens), otherwise the sum is incorrect.\n- If a column sum is 10 or more, you carry the extra to the next higher place (e.g., if tens sum were [math]12[/math], write [math]2[/math] in tens and carry [math]1[/math] to hundreds).\n\nConclusion:\nUnderstanding place values ensures correct alignment of digits and accurate addition of multi-digit numbers.",

        "question": "In what way does the process of finding the total number of students attending an event compare to adding monetary values?",
        "solution": "Solution:\n\nApproach:\n- Both finding the total number of students and adding monetary values use the same basic operation: addition of individual contributions. Key principles are the commutative and associative laws of addition and consistency of units.\n\nEssential steps:\n1. List each contribution (number of students from each group, or each monetary amount).\n2. Make sure all quantities are in the same unit (students or a single currency and denomination).\n3. Add the numbers together, grouping as convenient.\n\nWorking example:\n- Suppose groups have [math]12[/math], [math]18[/math], and [math]20[/math] students.
